WASHINGTON. Dec. 11.â In ths
House to-day a resolution
was adopted for the appolnt
,ment of r. special committee
of five members to Investi
gate the death of Oscar L. Booz of Bris
tol, Pa., which .occurred recently as a
result of hazing received while a cadet
at West. Point. This course was taken
over the head of the Military Committee,
which reported in favor of allowing the
War Department to conduct the inquiry.
When the House met to-day Hull, chair
man of the Military Affairs Committee,
reported back from that committee a sub
stitute for the Drigga resolution for an
Investigation of the alleged "hazing" of
Booz at West Point. The substitute Is as
follows:
"Wheroas.lt Is alleged In the newspapers
that Oscar L. Booz of Bristol. Pa., for
merly a cadet at the United States Mili
tary Academy at West Point, died at hl3
home from injuries purporting to have
been the result of hazing Inflicted upon
him by certain unknown cadets of the
said military academy: and
"Whereas, The Secretary of War is now
making Investigation of the facts,
"Resolved, That scid Secretary is re
quested to report the result of such in
vestigation to the House of Representa
tives, together with such recommenda
tions as he may see fit to make."
The report of Secretary Root, together
with that of Superintendent Mills, Is at
tached to the resolution. The presenta
tion of the resolution and the reading of
the reports of the Secretary of War and
Colonel Mills aroused great Interest in
the House.
Slayden of Texas, a member of the
Military Committee, expressed the opin
ion that the reports concerning hazing
at West Point had been grossly exagger
ated.
Clayton of NeVr York, who was himself
a graduate of West Point, defended the
Military Academy. He said there was no
necessity for bringing up the Dreyfua
case. The record of the academy stood
for itself.
"Why not Investigate, then?' asked
Wanger.
i "The academy fears no Investigation,"
replied Clayton. "The son of a washer
1 woman and a tuillionaire are on the same
footing when they enter the academy."
From his own personal experience Clay
ton said he knew that the reports of haz
ing were greatly exaggerated. The sen
timent of the cadets was against brutality
or doing anything unfair. Hazing as it
was practiced years ago, he said, had
been stamped out. â¢ -
, Hay of Virginia favored a Congressional
investigation not, he said, because he be
lieved there was any ground" for the
charges, but because an investigation
would vindicate the academy and the ca
dets.'
In concluding the debate Hull said he
had little faith in Congressional investiga
tions. It was erroneous to believe tha't
the army was interested in shielding
West Point, and he told what had been
done in the last five years to stop hazing.
"If the charges of hazing should be
proved," asked Driggs, "is there any law
which could reach the cadets who par
ticipated?"
"The gentleman can answer that ques
tion as well as I." answered Hull. "But
if proved, If the War Department does not
take steps to dismiss the guilty ones from
the academy, Congress will."
Drlgg3 of New York, the author of the
original resolution, protested vigorousiy
against the adoption of the substitute res
olution, as it did no't include provision for
an investigation of the general subject of
hazing at West Point with a view of put
ting a permanent stop to such practices.
He declared that the practice of infam
ously hazing "plebcs" at West Point was
notorious. Driggs said that he would
be in favorof abolishing the academy it
these brutal practices coold not be stop
ped. {Applause.)
Wanger of Pennsylvania, who ap
pointed Cadet Booz, spoke In favor of a
Congressional investigation. He said he
did not presume to know the exact de
tails, but responsible persons had made
statements relative to the case which had
profoundly moved the people of the coun
try. The gross outrages known to have
been committed at the academy in the
past, he said, had aroused the conviction
that the officers of the academy and of
the army were so Jealous of the fair
name of the academy that they guarded
it as indiscreetly as the officers of France
guarded the Dreyfus scandal. He was
not opposed to receiving any Information
the Secretary of War had on the subject,
or to the proposed Inquiry by the War
Department, but he Insisted that only a
Congressional inquiry would satisfy the
country. Such an investigation would
bring out the facts and lead to action
which would forever prevent cadets being
the victims of hazing In the future. Wan
ger said that at the proper time be would
offer a substitute for the committees 'res
olution providing for an Investigation by
a special committee of five members of
the House who should have the power to
send for persons and papers, and If neces
sary proceed to West Point, and that the
committee should report within sixty
days. Wanger's resolution for a special
committee of five to investigate the case
was then adopted without division. The
Speaker appointed the following members
to constitute the committee: Marsh of
Illinois, chairman; Wanger of Pennsylva
nia. Smith of Iowa. Driggs of New York
and Clayton of New York.
MILLS MAKES FULL DENIAL.
Hazed by Cadets.
Refutes the Charge That Boos. Was
WASHINGTON. Dec. lL-Colonel Mill*.
superintendent of the Military Academy,
has entered the broadest denial, based
upon a full Investigation made by himself
of the report that the- lata Cadet Oscar
Booz was hazed and otherwise mistreated
at the academy. The Secretary of War
has transmitted Colonel Mills' report t >
the House Committee on Military Affairs
in answer to the House resolution on the
subject, and has accompanied it by the
statement that at the instance of Colonel
Mills he has appointed the board, con
sisting of Major-General Brooke, Colonel
Gillespie and Colonel Clous, to meet at
West Point on the 15th Inst., or as soon
as possible thereafter, to Investigate, noc
only the Booz case, but also the methods
employed at the academy to prevent haz
ing.
Colonel Mills' statement is quite long.
He says he had no personal knowledgo
of Booz, whose record at the academy he
submits to demonstrate that there was
no medical evidence of any mistreatment,
but that his resignation was attributed
to weak eyes and a poor record in stud
ies. Having carefully Questioned cadeta
who were classmates of Booz. Colonel
Mills finds that the cadet had no trouble
prior to August 6. 1S3S. when he had a
ttst fight, witnessed by six cadets, all of
whom are now at the academy. Two
classmates of Booz testified that the lat
ter invited them to attend the fight; that
he went to it and engaged in It willing
ly, and in the beginning was the ag
gressor. All the cadets testify that the
encounter was short, of little severity
and Booz was In no way Injured or
marked. 'â¢
On the testimony of the cadets Colonel
Mills al3o denied specifically the story
that tabasco sauce was poured down
Booz's throat, and that be was called
"Bibles" or ridiculed on account of hU
religious belief. In conclusion he aakj
for the investigation which the Secretary
has ordered.

Arranging as a Basis for Serious Negotiations a , Short :: Preliminary
Protocol That Leaves Many Questions'- to Separate Conventions to.
Be Drawn Up Later ard Considered by the Interested Powers
LONDON, Dec. 12.â "Reports from
Chinese sources," says the
Shanghai correspondent of Â» the
Times, wiring Monday, "point to
the probability that serious nego
tiations for peace will begin forthwith. It
is believed that a short preliminary pro-
tocol is in process of arrangement, leav
ing many questions, including commercial
subjects, to separate conventions, to be
drawn up later.
"The powers Bhould retain their troops
in China until these questions are settled
and until tangible evidence Is given that
China honestly intends to fulfill her en
gagements. Progressive Chinese regard
the present 'as a great opportunity to over
throw the reactionary Manchu methods
and to Inaugurate a regime of national
progress.
"It Is urged that the powers shall insist
upon the cancellation of the claim of the
present heir-apparent, Prince Tuan's son,
and the substitution of an heir of full
age, free from any connection with the
Tuan party. Such a man is available,
and the step is declared to be legally pos
sible."

Sentenced to Be Hanged for the

TIENTSIN, Nov. 5. â The International
expedition to Paoting-Fu, after repeated
postponements, at length commenced
operations on October. 12, when, from Pe

taneously three columns.
The Tientsin columns were commanded

former taking a direct route to the north
of the waterway to Paoting-Fu and the
latter a more circuitous route to the
south of the same. At Machou, a small
walled city, on the 17th of October,. Gen-,
eral Campbell received a letter, from' Gen
eral Baellond, stating that . the French
had occupied Paoting-Fu on the 13th Inst;
without opposition, having anticipated
tho . actions of the allies and broken the
promise that they '.would await other
commands.

Paoting-Fu. s-1^'4

STRIKERS' PLACES ALL FILLED.
DENVER. Dec 11.â J. B. Comstock,
the day operator, and the night
operator, whose name is said to
toe Williams, were driven out of
Sedalia by unknown men at 8
o'clock to-night. Both are non-union
operators, filling the places of strikers on
the Santa Fe. Comstock was shot through
the upper portion of the right arm. The
wound is painful, but is not dangerous
unless blood poisoning should set In.
Three men of a crowd of seven men
loitering about the Santa Fe station at Se
dalia entered the room where Comstock
End Williams were Fitting. "Throw up
your hands," said one of the men draw
ing a revolver. Both operators obeyed
and while one man held a revolver the
other two searched the operators for wea
pons.
Williams was standing close to the
<5oor and while Comstock was being
Fcarched he bounded through the door.
The man with the revolver dashed aftfer
him and tired two shots. Williams dis
appeared in the darkness and has not
been heard of since. It is thought that
either he is hiding in the mountains or
that he was fatally wounded and died
Fome distance from the station. Comstock
believes Williams was shot.
After the escape of Williams Comstock
was ordered away. Comstock turned to
run out of the depot and the man with
the revolver shot him. He fell and the
men fied. A few minutes later a Colorado
and Southern train came in and its crew
found Comstock and he was taken to St.
Anthony's Hospital in Denver.
At Castle Rx>ck last night a party of
masked men took out Operator H. R. Wil
eon, tied a rope around his neck, led him
to the nearest trestle and grave him the
choice of jumping off or leaving town.
"Wilson walked to Sedalia. The mob
dosed the station and set the danger
cignals.
At Monument It is reported late to
night that the operator was driven out by
citizens. The non-union telegraphers at
Palmer Lake, two non-union operators
f r< in Kansas City, were driven away by
a crowd of men last night. They went
Quietly and were net hurt. As at each
placS where similar acts of violence have
been resorted to by the strikers and their
friends, the danger signals were set so
that trains would not run past the station.
Special detectives have been sent out
from Denver by the Santa Fe to hunt
down the men who attacked the non
union operators. At the headquarters of
the Order of Railway Telegraphers in this
city this statement was made. It was
fa id that the shooting of Comstock was
dnne by sympathizers with the strikers:
"The people are with us," said the head
cf the order here, "but we don't want
tb^m to go to shooting. At Palmer Lake
â¢nd Castle Rock the people will not feed
th* non-union operators and this is the
case at several places between Denver
End Pueblo."

MANAGER STODGE'S STATEMENT
Declares That the Strike Is a Com-
TOPEKA. Kans.. Dec. 11.â General Man
aper Mudge Fays that the Santa Fe strike
is a complete failure and that the road is
finding no difficulty in filling the places
made vacant by the operators. He still
maintains his place at the key in the gen
eral telegraph office, however. In an in
terview Mr. Mudge said to-night:
"Our business is running normal again.
We have kept train movements steady
under the train order system. It has been
satisfactory enough to run the trains in
the same way that other Western roads
run them. But the Santa Fe has block
signals, as the others have not. We are
using the block signals from Chicago to
Newton again. I gave the order for re
sumption this morning.
"The bogus message for operators to go
back to work, which J. A. Newman says
his name was forged to. went on the
wires sure enough. It was taken on* the
wire in the general office, at the shop and
at Holliday. Where it originated I don't
know. Mr. Newman's personal message
of protest reached me late yesterday
afterncon. There has been nothing further
heard about the matter since I replied last
night."

particularly uttentivo hearing.

went to the Committee on Agriculture.
This was a victory for the friends of tha
bill.
There also was a sharp debate over the
Montana Senatorshlo cas<e, but no action
was taken, the matter by consent going
over temporarily.
To-morrow no business session of the

devoted to the centennial of the estab
lishment of the seat of government In
Washington.
The resolution offered yesterday by
Hanna for the appointment by the presi

Senators to make the necessary' arrange
ments for the inauguration of the Presi
dent on the 4th of March was adopted
without debate.

Supplementary Fund of Sixteen Mil
lion Pounds Advanced.
LONDON, Dec. 11.â When the House cf
Commons went into committee of the sup
ply to-day William St. John Broderick,
the Secretary of Staie for War. intro
duced the supplementany estimates of
Â£16,000,000 for the army. He admitted that
the Government's expectations had not
been oversanguine and that it anticipated
that the expenditure to March 1 would
be little short of the outlay the Govern
ment had been incurring during the whole
fiscal year. He added that the Govern
ment's policy was to pursua the war wl'h
the strongest possible forco -and a con
tinuous stream of supplies so that peaco
may the more quickly be achieved Mr
Broderick added that he believed the Govl
ernment was now asking for the final sum
required for this abnormal war.
Sir William Vernon Harcourt. Liberal
expressed the opinion that the House had
not heard a more distressing confession.
Although his original opinion of the cir
cumstances leading to the unhappy war
was not altered he would oppose the vote
The whole secret of the Government's
miscalculations, he said, was this: It was
in no wise a conflict of governments, but
a. conflict of races. He thought that now
they had overthrown the governments
of the republics by overwhelming force
of arms they should deal with the brave
burghers remaining, not by perpetually
increasing the expenditure but by a noi
lcy of reconciliation.
Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman said
that the Secretary of State for War had
made a frank statement as to the new
condition of affairs, and he expressed a
hope that the Government would bring
home the volunteers. Mr. Broderick re
plied that It would be Impossible for the
Government to give any pledge on that
point, as Lord Kitchener must be sup
pled with many mounted men.
Timothy Healy remarked: "A war be
gun in braggadocio and hypocrisy has
brought humiliation to England, and the
culprit is In the Colonial Omce."
Henry Labouchere expressed the opin
ion that the guerrilla warfare In South
Africa might last for two years. The
supplementary estimate of Â£16,000,000 was
then adopted by a vote of 284 against 8
The House having gone Into Committee
of Ways and Means, the Chancellor of the
Exchequer, Sir Michael Hicks-Beach ex
plained that the existing borrowing pow
ers of the Chancellor amounted to only
Â£5,000,000. He moved, therefore, a resolu
tion authorizing him to borrow by war
loan exchequer bonds or treasury bills
Â£11,000.000. He said he did not expect to
use his borrowing powers until February
or March, and he asked the House to
postpone any discussion on the subject
until the third reading next week.
Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman ex
pressed his approval of the . resolution
which was agreed to.- The committee"
then. arose and the House adjourned.

What the Imperial Chancellor had said

cellor.

"I cannot admit that the American
President spoke about tariff retaliation as

still existing American prohibitory meas
ures."
He went on to advise members of all

At a special meeting of the shareholders
of the Grand Trunk Railroad to-day, the
purchase of the Chicago nnd Grand Trunk
Railroad was ratined. Sir Charles Rivers
Wilson, president nf the Grand Trunk,
maintained that the new arrangement
would reduce the obligations SGO.000 a year,
and enablp the whole length of the line
to be double tracked within a-short time.
He paid a g'owing tribute to the former
general manager of the Grand Trunk, <\
M. Hays, saying It was Impossible to find
a second Hays, but, he added, he believed
George Bell Reeve, the former traffic
manager of the Grand Trunk Railroad
and now the general manager, was the
best man whose services could be ob
tained. He dilated upon the sacrifice Mr.
Reeve had made In giving up his retire
ment to take on the onerous duties of
general manager.
The shareholders congratulated the di
rectors on the action taken, and grate
fully bid farewell to Mr. Hays. It was
announced at the meeting that Mr. Hays'
staff will all remain with Mr. Reeve.

DUBLIN, Dec. 11.â Delegates to the
number of several thousand representing
County Councils, borough corporations,
district councils, branches of the United
Irish League and kindred bodies met to
day In national convention in the Dublin
Rotunda to "recreate the Irish party."
John Redmond, who presided, said- it
was the "parliament of the Irish people."
Seventy-five Irish members of the .House
of Commons were present and several
priests were on the platform.
John Dillon, amid cheers, proposed a
resolution to the effect that the South Af
rican war v/as entered upon In pursuance
of a conspiracy to deprive two free na
tions of their liberty, in the interest of
capitalists and" mine owners.
He read letters from Boer generals giv
ing instances of the alleged brutality of
British soldiers In .South Africa, and also
the death notice of a Boer boy of eight
In the camp at Port Elizabeth, whoso
father had refused to surrender and
whepp home was blown up with dynamite-.
According to the notice, the child and his
mother were deported hundreds of miles
and fed upon pea soup. The lad's death
Mr. Dillon described as "cowardly mur
der." Intensely excited by Mr. Dillon's
speech, the assembly broke forth into
tierce cries and protests against the con
duct of the war.
The resolution was adopted unanimous
ly and the convention ordered it tele
graphed to the president of the Afrikan
der Bund. - 'Â¦-Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦
William O'Brien Introduced a resolution
excluding Timothy M. Healy from the
Parliamentary party. While their differ
ences are- of a personal character, he
urged that for the cake of unity Mr.
Healy's exclusion was necessary. â¢
Timothy Harrington, who was frequent
ly Interrupted. Bald that If the action of
the delegates plunged the country Into the
vortex of despair future generations
would curse the convention and those who
participated in it.
"Hogwash!" yelled an O'Brienlte Jn the
gallery.
"I know where the hoar is," said Mr.
Harrington, .which caused protests and
cries of "He Is calling his fellow patriots
'hogs.' "
.Mr. Harrington, after the chairman had
admonished the convention to give him a
fair hearing, made a temperate appeal for
harmony, i
After other speeches, an amendment
taking the bitterness out of the resolution
was proposed by Father McNeeee, but
this was rejected overwhelmingly, and
Mr. O'Brien's resolution adopted, only
twenty-five hands showing against it.
The convention then adjourned until to
morrow.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK; Dec. U.â By means of an
Invention in which a magnetic electric
machine is utilized 'to develop an induced
current of high potential, capable of act
ing over a long distance or through strong
resistance, there has been developed a
fystem of electric signaling which, by dis
pensing with cumbersome and costly bat
teries, bids fair to revolutionize modern
telegraphy and materially lessen its cost.
Another application Jf the principle of the
invention adds to the ordinary fire alarm
system safety, surety and economy. \As
heretofore developed, the current has not
been available for long distances or
against strong resistance, and hence not
commercially practical except In a small
way. a
To L,. G. Woolley of Kenton. Olilo. who
i5, secretary of the National Magneto-
S. tric i TeIe Â£rnph Company, Is due the
credit for perfecting the application of
â¢ . P ri , nci Pte involved, and the commer
cial utility of -which has been shown to
the satisfaction of many electric experts.
In the offices of the company in Park
Row Dr. Louis E. Nile*, president, to-day
showed the operation of the Invention by
telegraphing through a resistance equiva
lent to that of 1800 miles of wire. Ten
t nonsnnrl volts were used..

"Some members of the minority of the

Swanson nt Virginia, a member of the

He said there should be a reduction

Swanson asserted that the on!y persons

In conclusion Swanson stated that the
minority woujd move to recommit the bill

Special Dispatch to The Call.
PACIFIC GROVE, D*2. 11.â The new
museunv.of natural history that has Just
been established in this city was formally
opened to the public to-night with appro
priate exercises. Members of the museum
association and their friends assembled
at the museum lecture room, at 8 o'clock,
where the lady members held a reception.
The exercises followed and consisted of
an address by the president of the associa
tion. Thomas W. Cowan, upon the history
of the association during its year of work
preparatory to establishing the museum;
an address by Professor Josiah Keep, the
well-known' conchologist, and by Rev. O.
W. Lucas, trustee of th-) Pacific Grove
schools.- A collation was then served,
after which 'the company repaired to the
museum proper, where the curator, Miss
M. E. B. Norton, presented the mounted
and classified specimens to the public view
for the first time.
This museum has been a pet project of
California scientists for a number of
years and I Its establishment is causing
great interest throughout the State. Mon
terey " County , being the type locality of
nearly, all the Calif omla flora . and many
of the fauna, also, it seemed especially fit
ting that there should be a museum here.
There are about 2100 specimens already In
the museum, largely of scÂ» life, both ani
mal and vegetable. Including one of the
finest collections of. starfish and sea ur
chins on the coast and a valuable collec
tion of Monterey County, plants.
Contains a Valuable and Interesting
Collection of the Flora and
- , Fauna of Monterey
County.

ADRIFT MANY HOURS
IN WATERLOGGED BOAT
Terrible Experience of John Broder
ick, a Prospector, During a
Storm on Kotzebue
â¢ Sound.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CAHTO, Dec. 11.â News reached here by
the overland stage driver of the awful
experience of a prospector adrift In a
waterlogged boat seventy-two hours. The
steamer Selma, which arrived at Fort
Bragg yesterday, had on board a passen
ger from Skaguay, Alaska, named John
Broderick. Mr. Broderick had a terrible
experience on Hodum Inlet, Kotzebue
Sound. He was shipwrecked, and for
seventy-two hours was adrift in a water
logged boat, having 'no assistance what
ever and without anything to eat.
Broderick left tho coast about a year
ago with two other prospectors and sailed
for tho Kotzebue country. Having pros
pected that country for some time with
out having any, marked success, he, with
ten others, left for Point Hope, arriving
there about the middle of November, and
not* finding what they expected they
coasted along: .the shore beaded f or , the
Kowak River Â¦ country. After being out
eeveral weeks a storm came up and five of
the boats were blown ashore. Broderlck'a
boat being without, sails made slow pas
sage,' and the fury "of the storm became
such that he found himself unable to get
to shore, and he was soon drifting help
lessly about in the Inlet.
He was alone and the storm raged bo
heavy that he could" do nothing at all
with his boat. - The (second morning It
was capsized. After hours of hard labor
and suffering he succeeded In righting It,
and for several . hours remained on his
knees in the stern of the boat with ' only
his head above water. The boat was tossed
about by the water, : and when the storm
abated , Broderick Â¦"Â¦ drifted ashore, where
he was found several hours later, uncon
scious, by passing prospectors. *

Interesting Annual Meeting of the California. Press Association
In the Jinks Hall of the San Francisco Press â¢ Club.
r unjustly, to refer in some measure to
ne Stanford Institution.
"I am opposed,' 1 said President Wheeler,
to the Institution that represents the
annual meeting: of the California
Press Association. The Jinks hall of the
Press Club was placed at the disposal of
the out-of-town newspaper men and they
kept It full and lively until long after the
meeting was over In the evening.
The depression In Journalism was bal
anced by a stir In the collegiate atmos
phere created by some words of Presi
dent Benjamin Ide Wheeler of the State
University in a discussion of the Influence
of tho press. He and President David
Starr Jordan of Stanford University were
to discuss the question, but President
Wheeler attended the session of the after
noon and spoke then, while President Jor
dan attended In the evening. In his re
onarks President Wheeler , spoke of the
need of freedom in both a university and
a newspaper and he decried both institu
tions when they represented but one
man's Idea, but one man's plan and but
one man's thought. A year ago his words
would have passed as general; in view of
the Ross lnoident they were taken. Justly
THERE was a lull In Journalism
yesterday while the country edi
tors laid down their pencils and
flocked to the city to attend the
It was supposed that President Jordan
might make some reference to the Idea
of one thought and one laea in a uni
versity as .well as in a newspaper, but he
did not, except to say that it sometimes
brought to a paper a certain authority to
have it known that its columns expressed
always the views and sentiments of one
man, particularly If that one is a man
whose views are clear and well consid
ered. President Jordan's address was
rather a running criticism of the elements
of a newspaper as they appear to a
reader. "A newspaper is an educator,"
he said, "and in current things people are
In*, not one person's plan of living; it
should seek the truth wherever the truth
lies and it ahould . fallow . wherever the
truth leads. -
"A newspaper which recognizes the
value of giving things as they are be
comes not a subject of private ownership
but a public trust. "We like to have the
papers readable: we like a story, but we
want to know what the facts are. THere'
should be some privacy in the world, but
I believe what the public ought to know
should be told to the reporter. When
those who know will not tell reporters
must get their information from second
ary sources, and they cannot be blamed
If it be not accurate. The goal toward
which the newspaper Is progressing â¢ Is
that of an accurate organ of publicity.
In your sacred duty to your State and to
your political system you must regard
your positions as editors and reporters as
public trusts." â¢

Special Dispatch to The CalL
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Dec 1Lâ
An important addition was made to the
Stanford latr library to-day by the re
ceipt of S500 volumes of reports. ThÂ«
books were received direot from Minne
apolis, where a complete working library
was purchased last summer by Mrs. Stan
ford upon the recommendation .of Dr.
Nathan Abbott, the head of the Stanford
law school. The set. completes the record
of English cases and gives Stanford/ one
of the best working libraries in the coun
try, y
Dr. Abbott, dean of the law school, had
the following to say concerning this ad
dition to the facilities of his department:
"I am more than gratified by the re
ceipt of such a valuable collection of law
books. The set practically contains all
the English and Canadian reports. Some
of the volumes are rare and costly and
would easily bring $50 apiece in the open
market. The books will also be useful to
students in the history and economics de

In comparing the newspaper and the

demic freedom Is always to be found In
the colleges of this country. The same
sort of freedom a university likes to feel
a newspaper likes to feel; but freedom of
thought and freedom of speech sometimes
become carelessness of thought and care
lessness of speech. The more shining a
mark may be the more some papers wish
to throw mud at It. These are the real
enemies of free speech."
President Jordan then commented light
ly on some of the Journalistic frailties and
closed with his thanks for the privilege of
addressing the association. He was fol
lowed by. S. M. Haskell of tho Pomona
Progress,- who spoke of the newspaper

STANDARD OIL
WINS OHIO CASE
LOS ANGELES, Dec. 11. â John E. Fra
sier was arraigned to-day and L. L. Bte
vens, who at present is in Bakersfield, is
wanted, to answer to the charge of Bteal
ingr 230 barrels of crude petroleum from
"William Dryden of L'niversity. But this
theft, according: to a number of Oil men
who have been victimized and the investi
gation of detectives, is only one of a series
of similar larcenies. A conspiracy has
been unearthed which would have caused,
large losses to the oil producers if the
very boldness of the thieves had not
worked their undoing:. As it Is the oil men
believe that at leas.t 20u0 barrels, valued
at over $2wO, of their product have been
stolen and sold by Frasier and Stevens.
In every instance the petroleum was
taken from unlocked receiving tanks. The
thieves operated in two distinct methods.
That aJleged to have been adopted by Ifra
eier was to hire the drivere of oil tank
wagons to go at 2 or 3 o'clock in the morn
ing to some receiving tank, Frasier rep
resenting to them that he had a rush or
der thai must be tilled at once. For such
tripe he was willing to pay special rates.
Frasier would meet the teamsters at the
tank, which, not being guarded and being
far from the wells, was practically at the
mercy of the thief. When the wagxm tank
was filled be would direct the driver to
the Oil Storage and Trans po nation Com
pany's warehouse, where the oil was de
livered and in perfectly good faith credit
ed to Frasier's account. Recently Frasier
received $300 on account for oil left with
tho company for sale.
Stevens for some time acted as engineer
for Martin & Company's pumping plant
end Is eaid to have sold purloined oil di
rectly to consumers. He made his cus
tomers themselves come to the receiving
tanks, gave them other people's oil and
received cash on delivery. .
Frasier was held in default of $2000 bail
and a detective left for Bakersfield to-day
to arrest Stevens.

STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Dee. 11.â
Manager Glldersleeve received a reply to
day to his -telegram sent last Thursday
to the management of the Columbia foot
ball team. Gildersleeve was asked to
name the terms which he would offer the
Eastern team should they come to Cali
fornia during the holidays. This seems
to be a withdrawal by Columbia from
the agreement made some weeks ago to
make the trip for three-fourths of the
gross gate receipts and leaves the matter
bo uncertain that it is hardly likely the
two managers will bring their teams to
gether for a holiday game.
In addition to this the faculty athletic
committee has refused to allow the var
sity to make the Loa Angeles trip, bo a
second game with the Columbia team
would be impossible. As the men were
looking to the southern trip for what lit
tle pleasure they would get out of three
weeks of hard training they are not now
to willing ; to don their suits again. The
committee dealt more kindly with the
second eleven men,, granting permission
for them to Journey south arid meet the
Pomona, San Bernardino. San Diego and
Los Angeles Athletic Club teams.
Student Treasurer H. II. Taylor made
his final report to-day of the receipts of
the big game. It- shows that the total re
ceipts for the two colleges were $25,105,
of which Stanford's net- receipts were
$10,321, an excess of $2000 over last year's
game.

The wives of Commander Seton Schroe
der,\ Governor of Guam, and other offi
cers, who went to the island on the
Solace, remained at Guam.
The United States hospital ship Solace
has arrived at.Cavite from San Francisco.
Her officers say the condition of affairs
at the island of Guam has considerably
improved since a week ago. Dwellings
are being restored and the people are re
suming their occupations, but the crop3
are", practically all destroyed, though
there is no immediate want The Solace
left supplies there and the Arethusa has
taken a considerable quantity of supplies
to Guam from Cavite. The reports as to
the number of deaths are about un
changed.
In addition to this engagement there had
been several minor encounters between
the troops and the insurrectionists.
'Â¦' MANILA, Dec. 11.â A detachment of the
Fifth Cavalry had a fight with a hundred
insurgents south of Santa Cruz Sunday.
The insurgents were chased for four
miles. Fourteen of them were found
dead. There were no American casual
ties.

For a boy who rides many good races,
Johnny Woods can also supply some
rancid exhibitions of stupid horsemanship.
He had the mount on St. Cuthbert in the
six-furlong spin and had he gone on about
his business the horse's backers might
have cashed. Instead, with his mount
only galloping into the stretch, he allowed
O'Connor, astride the favorite, ineory, to
pull up on even terms. Then he drew his
whip and at the wire lost out rather easily
by a neck. Rides like this one keep peo
ple guessing, and there Is little wonder at
it. Lennep. a polished mud performer,
lost the show to Good Hope.

DOMINICK'S FINE SADDLE WORK
CALLS FORTH UNSTINTED PRAISE
Compelled to Stand In Line for
Meals and Then to Sit
on a Wet Deck to
The transport Hancock's load of sick
and wounded soldiers was transferred
yesterday from the vessel to the Presidio.
The general appearance of the men was
much better than that presented by pre
vious shiploads. The passenger list con
tained the names of 213 soldiers and of
this number seventy-six were found fit
for Immediate discharge. The rest were
distributed throughout the various wards
of the general hospital.
The usual complaint of poor food and
poor manner of handling those soldiers
who were not sick enough to be entitled
to berths In the ship's hospital was to be
heard from the men. The 6oldiers claim
that they were not furnished the 40 cents'
worth per day of food which they are al
lowed and that the stuff which was given
'them was not fit to eat. They were also
fed like pigs, so they claim, no tables be
ing furnished them. It was a casa of stand
In line and take what was handed to
you and then find a spot on the deck clean
enough and dry enough upon which they
could sit to dispose of it. Most of the
men who returned were convalescing
from attacks of dysentery and the food
furnished was not of me kind usually
given 6lck men. In the event that a man
was unable to stomach the meat fur
nished, he could make his meal upon soda
crackers, provided he could get them.
The principal Item on the bill of fare
was a sort of stew, in soldier parlance
called "slum." This was to be had morn
ing, noon and night and It Is a matter of
record that at no stage of the voyage was
a potato or other vegetable discovered In
the mixture.
Among the Insane brought back on the
Hancock was Acting Assistant Surgeon
George Â¦%%'. Storey of Portland, Or. The
young man's mania is of a suicidal nature
and a close watch must be kept upon him.
Borne time before the young doctor's mal
ady made it necessary to confine him he
attempted to drown himself in the Passig
River and was only saved through the
heroic action of a sergeant of the Third
Artillery.
Captain F. S. Dewey, assistant surgeon,
LT. S. V., returned on the Hancock and
has been ordered home for discharge on
account of disability.
Acting Assistant Surgeon Relpsnyder,
one of the Hancock's medical staff, has
been ordered to return to the islands upon
the expiration of one month's leave of
absence.
Clara M. Zeller and Llbbie Wilson, fe
male'nurses, will return after a short
rest. Nurse Lucy G. Annan has been or
dered home for annulment of contract.
Sergeant Wunderllch of Battery C,
Third Artillery, was thrown from his
horse yesterday and severely injured.
When picked up he was unconscious and
found to be suffering from a badly cut
Jaw and numerous bruises on the head.
He was treated at the post hospital.
Harry McCann, a private of Company A,
Twenty-first Infantry, died yesterday at
the general hospital.
Twelve insane, soldiers were sent yester
day to the Government Hospital for the
Insane at Washington. Among the lot
was the colored soldier, Griffin, who is
very violent, and over whom the gruard
in charge has orders to watch most care
fully. Several times while in the insane
ward at the Presidio hospital he broke
loose, demolishing the furniture and in
juring the attendants.
All available casuals and assigned re
cruits now at the Presidio, belonging to
organizations 6erving In the Philippine
Islands or China, will be held in readiness
to sail for "Manila on the army transport
Logan about the 15th inst.
Captain "Warren H. Cowlea, Fourth In
fantry, now at the Presidio, is designated
for service with and to accompany the de
tachment.

The report attracted the attention of
those in Lyons' bar. Among them were
Colonel" Al Andrews and a man known
as 'Chalky Jack." both bookmakers?, the
latter in the employ of Humphries & Co.
They seized the man. who was brandish
ing the smoking pistol, and attempted to
wrest It from his grasp. He eluded them,
however, and ran up toward Stockton
street. At the corner he ran into the
arms of Policemen AV. Proll and C. E
Kennell. He refused to relinquish the pis
tol, which while running he had put into
his hip pocket. In the scuffle to gain pos
pession of it another chamber exploded,
the bullet narrowly missing his foot. At
the station Smith seemed extremely ex
cited and refused to talk about his act
lie was well dressed and appeared to
have been drinking: None of the per
sons concerned claim any knowledge of
Smith and declare he was a perfect
stranger. It Is said Smith is a well-known
tenderloin character.
The Summers woman had driven down
to Lyons' place in a hack with an escort,
and after Indulging In liquid refreshments
they re-entered the hack. As they took
their seats Smith, who had been concealed
in the shadow of some building on the op
posite side of the street, got into the hack,
telling tho driver to drive fast, and that
he was a doctor and in trouble. The
hackman ordered Smith to get out of the
hack, which he did after protesting. He
walked a few feet away and then, draw
ing a revolver, opened fire on the occu
pants of the vehicle.
lodged in the Central Police Station. He
later bailed himself out by putting up
$50. The charge preferred was that
of carrying a concealed weapon, the wea
pon In question being a murderous-look
ing 41-caliber revolver with two chambers
empty.
Military Reunion.
Company P of the First Regiment In
fantry of the National Guard had a mili
tary reunion in the Ellis-street armory
last night for the entertainment of mem
bers and invited guests. There were pres
ent a large number of ladies and their es
corts, including many military men. Prior
to the dancing, musical and literary num
bers â¢were contributed by Miss Lulu
Magee, Edgar Goldsmith, "Will Locke, Ray
McClinton and Kenneth A. Millican. Cor
poral William Cohn was floor manager
and J. A- Eason assistant. They were
assisted by Sergeant C. D. Cook, Corporal

Vitriol-Thrower at Large.
The police have thus far been unable to
locate the fiend who threw vitriol on the
dresses of the Bass sisters and their cou
sin, Margaret Tuckey, while they were
on their way to St. Brigld's Church, last
Sunday. Chief Sullivan is satisfied that
the vitriol thrower is a crank who has
an aversion to children's dresses.
"It's nobody's business who the woman
Is," remarked- Seymour. "The diamonds
have been returned and that is all there
is to it."
For some unknown reason, the head of
the detective department refused to reveal
the name of the woman who lost the dia
monds or of the man who found them.
A woman, whose name Captain of Do
toctlves Seymour refuses to' divulge, while
walking along Montgomery street last
Saturday afternoon, dropped a box con
taining diamonds worth $11,000. She ad
vertised her loss In the newspapers offer
ing a liberal reward for their return.
Yesterday the man who found the pre
cious stones visited police headquarters*
and turned them over to Captain Sey
mour.

A tenderloin shooting scrape Involving
many of, the prominent characters of that
district occurred outside the saloon, of
Billy Lyons on Post street early yester
day morning. The target was Rosie
Summers, better known as "Russian
Rosie," and the wielder of the revol
ver, a man claiming to be a doctor by

Claiming to Be Physician.
FIRES AT NOTORIOUS WOMAN.
Rosie Summers' Life Sought by Man
After firing the shot the woman, think
ing that she had seriously wounded her
husband, ran from the house toward Mar
ket street, where she was arrested. While
she was being placed in the patrol wagon
Pickering begged to be allowed to ac
company her to the Southern station.
"Take that, you scoundrel," shouted the
woman, as she aimed the pistol at her
guilty husband and fired. Fortunately for
him her aim was bad and the bullet did
no further damage than to make a hole
in his trousers.
Driven to desperation by the man who
had led her to the altar, Emma Pickering,
aged 22 years, last night made an attempt
to murder him. Discovering her husband
in a room In the Inglewood House, at 156
Fifth street, with another woman, Mrs.
Pickering fired a shot at him, but did no
more damage than to tear away a portion
of his trousers.
Pickering conducts the Bristol restau
rant at 126 Fifth Btreet- He has been
married for about five years, and has two
children, one being -two years old and the
other about fourteen months.
About two weeks ago Pickering left his
wife and children and went to live with
a woman who is known as "Alice", at
the Inglewood House. After vainly
searching for him Mrs. Pickering last
night learned that he had left his place
of business shortly after 8 o'clock, pre
sumably In the company of the woman
called ""Alice." Mrs. Pickering, goaded
to desperation, went to her home and
taking a revolver wi.'ch belonged to her
husband determined to kill him. After
kissing her babies "good-by" the dis
tracted woman with the pistolâ a cheap
one of thirty-eight caliberâ in her pocket
hurriedly repaired to the house where
her husband and his female companion
were stopping. Accosting the clerk she
learned the room in which her husband
was and with murder in her eyes rapped
for admittance. Pickering, recognizing
his wife's voice, refused to admit her.
After roundly cursing her he opened the
door and asked his wife what she wanted.
Wronged Wife Driven to Desperation at Detection of Hus
band's Fickleness Seeks Revenge With Loaded Pistol.

and enjoys the well-merited reputation of
being a wit and a beauty.

"Having work in my department of an
experienced nature, I desire to ask that
you allow me eight experienced clerks, as

You have sent me clerks who are thor
oughly unfamiliar with the duties re
quired of them, with but two exceptions.
I make this request at the suggestion of
one of the ordinary clerks that men of ex
perience should perform this particular
work."

The appointment of Professor W. W.
Campbell as Dr. Heeler's successor as di
rector of Lick Observatory, made recently
by the Lick Observatory committee, was
formally ratified. The new director will
enter upon the discharge of the duties of
the office January L
"The choice," said President Wheeler,
in reporting the action of the comrrlttee.
"has practically been made for us by the
world's eminent astronomers. Letters
came to t:s from all over the world, and
but one sentiment was expressed â 'Your
choice is easy, the man who is now your
acting director.' These letters constitute
a wonderful set of credentials."-
The regents reconsidered their action of
some weeks ago, and voted the widow of
the late Director Keeler the salary which
would have been due him to January 1.
The amount which she will receive as a
result of this action Is $1537 65. President
Wheeler recommended that the salary to
January 1 be paid to Mrs. Keeler, and
General Barnes spoke eloquently in favor
of the recommendation. He eaid:
"Lick Observatory, splendid as it is. is
a helpless machine except it have the
guidance of a master mind, pre-eminent
in his department of science; a man de
voted not to money-getting and who lays
up his treasures in the heavens eternal.
Such a man was Professor Keeler. Prom
inent men from all parts of the world who
visited California have been anxious to
see Lick Observatory. We have sent
them to Mount Hamilton with letters of
introduction to Director Keeler. and we
of the committee were often his guests.
So it was that he was called upon fre
quently for generous entertainment. It
all came out of the pocket of our dead
friend.
"We really owed him a money consider
ation, and when we said to the widow
that we would pay it to her, we did only
what we had a right to do. I made the
original motion, and when I learned, after
a later meeting, that the action had been
repealed, the gift taken back, I was in
finitely chagrined and pained. This gift
was not the misappropriation of a trust
fund, but the payment of a just debt."
The motion was passed by a unanimous
vote.
Judge Wallace called attention to the
fact that three months' pay had been,
voted the widow of the late Secretary
Bonte at the same time as the gift to
Airs. Keeler had been made, and the ac
tion had been rescinded with it He
moved that three months' payâ $S25â be al
lowed Mrs. Bonte. and made an earnest
plea In her behaif. Judge Slack asked
that the motion be laid on the table until
the next meeting, to give opportunity for
Inquiry as to certain points, and with Re
gent Wallace's cpnsent the motion was
tabled.
The revised Benard plans were expected
to arrive from Paris, and were to be sub
mitted to the repents for final adoption,
but they were delayed en route from Chi
cago. Regent Reinstein announced that
they would probably arrive to-night. It
was decided to Hold a special meeting Fri
day at 2 p. m. for the inspection of the
plans.
President Wheeler made a report, and
upon his recommendation votes of
thanks were given as follows: To the
Hon. D. O. Mills for his offer to defray the
expenses, to the amount of J24.WU, of a two
years' astronomical expedition from the
Lick Observatory to an observing station
south of the equator, for the study of the
movement of etars in the line of sight;
to Mrs. Jane K. Sather for her gift of $10.
(00 outright for the establishment of the
Jane K. Sather library fund; to William
R. Heaxst for Mb gift of a collection of
Greek and Kgyptian antiquities; to the In
dependent Klectrlc Light and Power Com
pany for its gift of a WestinRhouse in
duction motor, to be used in tne depart
ment of mechanics.

mology of the name California is uncer
tain. Some writers have pretended that
it was derived from two Latin words,
calida-fornax, or, in the Spanish lan
guage, caliente fornallaâ a hot furnace.
Michael Venagas, a Mexican Jesuit, in
"Natural and Civh History of Califor
nia," published in 1758. expresses a doubt
as to the correctness of this. In his opin
ion, the .early Spanish discoverers did not
name their new found land In this pedan
tic fashion. He wrote: "I am of the opin
ion that this name owed Its origin to some
accident; possibly to- some 'words spoken
by the Indians and misunderstood by the
Spaniards.' California was a-name given
by Cortez to the peninsula of Lower Cali
fornia, which he discovered in 1535 HÂ«
took the name from an old Spanish ro
mance in which there Is an imaglnarv isl
and. called California, abounding in great
treasures of gold The root of the word

CAPUCHIN VELVET DRESS.
BEAVER COLORED DRESS.
All that is to be asked is that the Department of
Agriculture be enabled to furnish each State with
four good models of highway, and surely that is not
asking too much. In the meantime the efforts of the
reformers will be continued to arousa the States and
the counties to go foiward in the work. . There ar<^
now many forces promoting the campaign of educa
tion on the subject, and it is fairly certain good re
sults will be obtained. California should be among the
foremost States of the Union fn the work of improve
ment, and the coming Legislature should provide
suitable legislation to promote it.
It will be perceived it is not the intention of tho
promoters of the movement to shift the burden and
the responsibility of constructing good roads from the
States and counties., to the Federal Government.
Such a plan is not feasible, and even if it were it
would not be desirable, for there could be no surer
way of postponing the establishment of good roads
than to make them dependent upon Federal aid. Un
der such a system States and counties would wait for
Federal aid, and very little would be done...
The plan now under consideration is to have the.
Federal Government construct one mile of model i
highway in each State. The mile is not to be con
tinuous, but is to be divided into four quarters, lo
cated in different parts of the State, the object of such
division being to show how roads should be con
structed under the different conditions of the various
localities, and thus make the object lessons more
widely useful than they would be otherwise.
GOVERNMENTAL ROAD WORK
1 \EPORTS irom Washington announce that the
Y â S promoters of the movement for improved high
ways throughout the United States have ar
ranged to urge upon Congress the advisability of
making this year to the Department of Agriculture
an appropriation of $150,000, to be used for the pur
pose of constructing model highways in various parts
of the country' for the double purpose of stimulating
road improvement and teaching how such improve
ments can be effected.
FASHION HINTS FROM PARIS.
The United States will build the canal and share
with France the glory she gained in cutting the isth
mus of Suez.
We beg to assure the Paris newspaper and the
French people that Mr. Hay's fellow citizens note no
such preference in him, and have nothing of the kind
to pardon or overlook. He is recognized here as a
sturdy American and an able diplomatist, who has
withstood all the Cabinets of Europe in the Chinese
matter and forced them to the American plan of set
tlement of that horrid situation. We rather incline
to the view that the Figaro's iil humor is due to the
passing of the last chance for the Panama ditch and
the prospect that the isthmian canal will follow the
Nicaragua route. France had the opportunity to dig
the canal, and an honorable administration of that
great work would have completed it long ago. We
cc not gird at that nation for its failure. The money
squandered would have finished the canal if honestly
applied. That it was not so applied France herself has
furnished the evidence. Ill-natured references to the
subject will onh' serve to recall the painful incidents
that clouded the close of the great career of Count At
Lesseps and tarnished the entire official character cf
France.
The Figaro's appe-il to American prejudice U
amusing rather than otherwise. It says: "The
American Secretary of Foreign Affairs (Mr. Hay) al
ready has need of much tact to make his fellow citi
zens pardon his taste for English society and for the
English Cabinet."
In this situation, if difficulty can be stirred up be
tween this country and England and delay caused
thereby, there might be a desperate chance for the
Panama route, which is free from all complications
arising in the Clayton- Buhver treaty.
France has no good feeling toward England just
now, and, on the general subject of an isthmian caiial,
has hoped that the United States would adopt the
Panama route and take that partly dug ditch off th;
hands of the French company.
AMERICANS will pay but little attention to the
comments of the Paris Figaro and other
French papers on the proceedings of the
American Senate on the Hay-Pauncefote canal treaty.
FRANCE AND THE CANAL.
SUGGESTION FOR UNION
OF MILK-WAGON DRIVERS
EDITOR OF THE CALL*â WH1 you kindly permit me, through the columns of
The Call, to bring the followjnsf matter to the attention of your readers â â¢specially
those who may be Interested In my suggestionâ to propose a movement to form an
organization of ml Ik- wagon drivers of ths city, which would give fraternal and social
advantages and privileges resulting in much good and great benefit to each and every
member.
The object of such an organization as I suggest should be to regulate wages,
shorten the hours of labor, wherever necessary, establish an employment bureau, pro
vide for the Eick and needyâ in short, to advance ar.d promote the welfare of each
number to mutual benefit and advantage.
The Initiation fee and monthly dues could be mad; so low that no obstacle be placed
In the way of all those desirous of Joining, thereby adding to strength and perma
nency.
Few there are. If any, who have not at one time or another felt the need of
organization. Long continued hours of labor with little rest make this occupation
one of the hardest, and thÂ«re are few who can withstand it for any great length
of time. In some instances the wages paid are nothing considering the time em
ployed.
If permission is kindly granted to use the columns of this paper to enable a few
of those Interested in this movement to be heard from on this subject and offer
such additional suggestions as may be proper here, we can get together and issue a
call for a meeting at an early date In order to dlscurs ways and means by which
it would lead to a permanent organization. Until others have been heard; from in
repard to this matter I shall defer further mention rntil later; but should be pleased.
If gjven support, at the proper time to tÂ£ke tho Initiative and see the thing
through successfully.
1 am a milk-wagon driver with eight years' experience in the city. '

PERSONAL MENTION.
y^ ARTOONISTS of European papers vary In their conceptions of Uncle
fl^ Sam in a manner reminiscent of the blind men who felt of the ele
(l . phant and based their opinions of the brute's appearance according
to the feeling of the particular part of the elephant's body_of which
each laid hold.
It Is only since the commencement of the Chinese Imbroglio that the for
eign cartoonists have had an opportunity of giving Uncle Sam a part in
their humorous or satirical conceptions of the situation that confronts the na
tions that have marched troops Into China, The old gentleman's habit of
minding his own business and not mixing in the quarrels of other nations is
largely responsible for the proneness of the continental caricaturists to ignoro
the homely figure which custom has created to be emblematic of the great
Yankee nation.
â¢ -Just now It is different.. Many of the cartoonists on the other side of the
water are trying an apprentice hand at delineating Uncle Sam. All of the
caricaturists possess features in common, and yet they differ widely. Col
lectively they spring palpably from one parent stemâ the good, old-fa^hloned
Uncle Sam that was born yoars ngo from the pencil of native cartoonists and
has flourished perennially ever since. 3pangled breeches, stove-pipe hat, chin
whiskers, cowhide boots and all.
Each foreign picture maker, however, allows his own personality to have
sway In nddlngr to or taking from the caricature. These changes are plainly
colored by local habit. A. look n.t the half-dozen examples of Uncle Sam
as seen by alien eyes, reproduced from leading foreign papers, proves that.
To the notions of the German Klatteradatsch artist. Uncle Sam is a German.
Russian characteristics are visible in the offering of the St. Petersburg Stre
kosa artist. On the pages of the Italian Papapallo. ho is an Italian: -while
the man who does cartoons for Le Charivari of Paris has Infused a jaunty,
Frenchy air into his countenance and attire.
The feasibility of the enterprise being proven, and
its importance being universally recognized, it is to be
hoped Congress will no longer hesitate in providing
for it. It is to be regretted the British will have the
honor of taking the lead in crossing the Pacific with
a cable, but we can be a close successor and ought
not to delay in the work.
The surveys of which the President speaks, and
which, as he says, "have shown the entire feasibility
of a chain of cables which at each stopping-place,
shall touch on American soil," have brought to light
irany interesting facts concerning the ocean bed be
tween Hawaii and the Philippines. According to the
report of Rear Admiral Bradford, there is an almost
level plain of soft mud, of a nearly uniform depth of
2700 fathoms, extending from Honolulu to the Mid
way Islands, and another about 500 fathoms deeper
between the latter and Guam, broken by submarine
reefs and mountain ranges. From a scientific stand
point one of the most interesting discoveries made
was that of a submarine mountain range about 500
knots from Guam, which apparently connects with
the one which extends from the coast of Japan to the
Bonin Islands. To the north of this range, according
to the report, the bed of the ocean slopes gradually to
the eastward into the great Japanese Deep, which for
years held the record for ocean depths.
Such a cable will be costly and will not yield the
commercial profits that would have been gained by
touching at Hawaii, Samoa and New Caledonia. The
fact that the British have sacrificed those commer
cial opportunities for the sake of having complete con
trol of the line shows how much they value such con
trol. To the United States exclusive control of a cable
is a matter of as much importance as to the British,
and, accordingly, the construction of the British line
may well be included r.mong the circumstances which
emphasize the need of one to our Government.
According to the reports the British enterprise is
to cost about $8,975,000. It is to start from Van
couver and will touch no land except that controlled
by the empire. Consequently, instead of making a
landing station at Hawaii, the cable will go direct to
Fanning Island, making by far the greatest span
known to ocean cables. From that point it will pas^
by Samoa to Fiji, and from there be carried to the
Australian coast, thus leaving New Caledonia out of
the connection.
Although the President did not say so, it is prob
able that among the circumstances which to his mind
emphasize the need of the cable is the fact that the
British are about to lay one across the Pacific which
will be completely under their control. It has been
announced from London that the imperial Govern
ment and the Governments of the various colonie3
concerned have accepted the'offer of a British com
pany to lay such a cable, and the work will be under-
pNRESIDENT McKINLEY in recommending to
V â y Congress the prompt construction of a Pacific
cable line recalled the fact that he had made a
similar recommendation in a special message of Feb
ruary 10, 1899, and added: "Since then circum
stances have strikingly emphasized this need. Sur
veys have shown the entire feasibility of a chain ot
cables which at each stopping-place shall touch on
American territory, so that" the system shall be under
our own complete control."

UTTERANCE
IN VARIETY
It will be seen that from the comparatively weak
position we held at the beginning of the century we
have now attained a position from which we can start
the new century as the leader of' the world in com
merce and industry. Nor can it be doubted that the
supremacy we now enjoy will be increased with the
years that are to come.

comparison of the exports of the whole group with
those of the United States .shows that the excess of
exports of the eighteen ' gives a grand total of only

are obtainable, while the excess of the United States

CUBA'S CONSTITUTION.
That body has, however, been working diligently
and intelligently in forming a fundamental law for the
new republic. The only serious issue before it has ap
peared in fixing the qualifications for the Presidency.
Three instruments have been presented to the conven
tion, framed respectively by Senors Rivera, Morua
and Quesada. Rivera's plan follows closely the con
stitution of the United States. It creates a govern
ment of three co-ordinate branches, executive, legis
lative and judicial. The and members of
the House of Representatives are to be elected by
popular vote. The present provinces will be called
departments, conforming to our States, and each will
be divided into municipalities, conforming to our
counties. The municipalities Will elect their own local
officers, and at the same time will choose delegates to
the department Legislature. The Senators will be
chosen by this Legislature.
Rivera's constitution provides that the President of
the republic must be a native of Cuba. This is in-
tended to shut out General Gomez, who is a San Do
mingan. His partisans are represented in Morua's
plan, which differs from Rivera's in providing as the
only qualification for President that he served in th
revolution. Information as to this clause in Morua's
plan is defective, inasmuch as we are not informed
whether it provides that the President must be either
a native or an alien who took part in the revolution.
This would provide for the temporary issue made over
Gomez, and thereafter pass the Presidency down the
native line. No information is furnished as to the
length of the Presidential term. Senators are to re
ceive a salary of $1500 per year, and each department
is entitled to four Senators, and one representative for
each 30,^00 people, providing that when the popula
tion exceeds 15,000 it shall be entitled to one represen
tive.
All plans seem to combine in providing for pay
ment of the debt to the army and navy contracted by
the late provisional government. There seems some
doubt about the acceptance of this by the convention.
The situation is analogous to our own Revolutionary
expenses, which had been provided for by the issue
of Continental money, which was never redeemed
after the adoption of our constitution in 1789. In
; deed, we are not aware that any such revolutionary
j debt has ever been paid, though there may be in
1 stances.
The fight of the Gomez party for Morua's plan is
described as "red hot," and if successful the San Do
mingan will probably be the first President. Other-
I wise a native Cuban, probably Rivera or Quesada, will
be chosen.
It seems to be understood that Bishop Sbarretti is
leading the anti-Gomez forces, and it is said that
Gomez has demanded of General Wood the removal
of the Bishop. It is riifticult to see how the military
authorities of the United States can separate a Bi?hop
from his see, but the request exhibits an element of
religious rancor in the Cuban situation. Under the
circumstances the Bishop would better abstain from
politics and leave the building of a civil government
to laymen. It bodes no good to the future of Cuba
tc mix a religious feud in the foundations of the
government.
All of the plans recognize religious toleration. They
: are at present in the hands of the sections of the
' central committee of the convention for stud}', and it
j ir hoped to have the perfected instrument ready for
1 reference to the present session of our Congress.
This reference becomes necessary under the terms
] and conditions of our declaration of war against
â¢ Spain. It is a novel event in our history that the con
; stitution of another republic is referred to our Gov
. ernment for ratification and indorsement. Under the
I plan it may be left for our Congress to decide upon
: the qualifications of the President of Cuba, and the
'red-hot fight over Gomez may. be transferred from
I Havana to Washington. It would appear at this di->
I tance that the Morua plan of limiting the Presidency
j to a native or a revolutionist will be the safer to
i adopt, considering the future peace of the island.
Gomez is a heroic figure in Cuba, and, if shut out by
the constitution, ill feeling will smolder to an extent
I threatening the peace and prosperity of the people.
It is the rule among men that the conqueror either
i becomes the ruler or establishes a line of rulers.
; Charlemagne was the grandson of Martel, who turned
; the tide of Mahometan conquest at Tours, and Wil
| liam became King of England after the victory at
i Hastings. We made Washington our first President,
I and Napoleon emerged from the revolution of 1793
' as First Consul and Emperor.
lost sight of.

public attention the meeting of the Cuban con
stitutional convention and its proceedings were

COMMERCE OF THE NATIONS.
| â * XPERTS of the Bureau of Statistics of the
Treasury Department have been for some time
! * * .past engaged in preparing a statistical abstract
showing the imports and exports of every country in
| the world having statistical records. The object of
! the abstract is to present in a single volume an ac
| count of the world's commerce, not only of to-day,
| but extending back over a long series of years, with
! details concerning t*ie principal articles of trade,
! country by country, with quantities and values ex
| pressed in our measurements and currency,
i The opening chapter of the work has been com
| pleted and in itself contains a large amount of valu
â¢ able information affording material for interesting-
I comparisons between the commerce of our own coun
; try and that of other nations. The imports for home
j consumption of the United Kingdom, for instance,
J which in the year 1800 amounted to $81,310,000,
! amounted in 1899 to $2,043,896,450, an increase of 2400
; per cent; while in the case of the United States the
; imports for home consumption in 1800 were $52,121,
j 891, and in 1899 $685,441,892, an- increase of 1215 per
; cent. Taking the export side the contrast is much
: more clearly in favor of the United States. The ex*
j ports of articles of home production from the
I United Kingdom in 1800 were $111,107,000, and in
I 1899 $1,287,151,345, an increase of 1059 per cent; in the
i case of the United States the exports of home
; products in 1800 were $31,840,903, and in 1899
j $1,203,931,222, an increase of 3681 per cent.
One of the most interesting features of this opening
chapter is the showing made of the relation of imports
I to exports in the various countries. The records 01
i forty-seven countries other than the United States are
! given in the tables, and from these it appears that
i eighteen countries show an excess of exports over im
j ports and twenty-nine show an excess of imports over
exports. Virtually all European countries; except
Russia, Am 1a and Spair^, show an excess of imports
over export?. In the other leading countries of the
world, with the exception of China and Japan, tjie
exports are in excess of imports.
A comparison of these figures shows in a ttriking
manner the pre-eminence of the United States in the
commercial world. Among the eighteen countrie*
whose exports exceed their imports are such great
populations as those of Russia, India, Austria, Spain,
When looking at Christmas goods don't, overlook
those of California production. The native article is
the proper novelty to send to your friends in theJEast^

receive Oom Paul, for now we will never know which
of the two thinks himself the bigger man and tries to
show it in conversation. â¢ . '.-. ' .';.

When a railroad provides watchmen to
guard employes from danger while at
work under or around a car. it perrorms
its whole duty and is not liable for an ac
cident caused by the negligence of the.
watchman in Jiot giving proper warning
to the workmen thus engaged. In a case
in Wisconsin where this rule was applied,
it was shown that there were three train
men standing by a car when the accident
happened to a man who was working un
derneath; and. furthermore, that "it was
the duty of each of them" as an incident
to his employment to act as a watchman
to protect his coemploye. The court re
marked that it was true there were no
â¢written or published regulations of the
company showing that they must thus
Watchmen to Guard Employes.
This principle that an employer must
make and promulgate rules, which, if
faithfully followed, will give reasonable
protection to his employes. Is a very gen
eral one; indeed, it prevails in every State.
Ar.d perhaps the rule is almost as well
known that rules adopted by the employes
that are not regularly put forth by their
employerâ which, therefore, they are not
required to obey â will not excuse the com
pany from doing Its duty.
Rules Made \yy Employes.
In Berrigan's case against the Erie Rail
road an employe of the- company was in
jured while coupling cars and died from
the injury. In the suit against the com
pany it was claimed that it had failed to
make and publish sufficient regulations
for the guidance of its empiaj' es while
coupling cars. The court in- considering
this claim remarked that it was easy
enough after an accident had occurred to
suggest how it might have been prevent
ed. The company was only bound to usÂ»e
ordinary care in formulating rules, and it
was not reasonable to proceed on the as
sumption that every injury to an employe
could be prevented by making such rules.
It was the duty of the company to anti
cipate and guard by rules or otherwise
against only such accidents as might rea
sonably be foreseen by the company.
There was no proof that rules for such a
case had ever been promulgated by any
other railroad, or that it was reasonable
or practicable to provide against the oc
currence of such an accident by a rule.
The question whether the company v.-as
at fault in omitting to make and publish
such a rule was left to the jury- "This."
remarked the Court of Appeals, "opened
to the jury a wide field for speculation
and conjecture. In the absence of some
proof on the part of the person who sued
the company that such a rule was in
operation by other roads. â¢ â¢ ? or
unless the necessity and propriety of mak
ing and promulgating such a rule was so
obvious as to make the question one of
common experience and knowledge, the
court is not warranted in submitting such
a question to the Jury." The court fur
ther thought that the rules in use pro
vided a reasonable way against the oc
currence of such an accident and conse
quently there was no case to present to
the Jury.
Berrigan vs. Erie Railroad.
In the case of Abel against the Dela
ware and Hudson Railroad, a car repairer
In the railroad's employ was under one of
Its cars making repairs. The car was
Btandlng on a sidetrack. Another car was
carelessly backed against it by other em
ployes BJid caused his death. In action
brought against the company by tho
man's wife to recover money for the loss
of her husband it was shown that other
railroad companies had adopted a rule for
g lacing a blu* flag by day and a blue light
y night on a car under which repair men
were at work and prohibiting the coup
ling or moving of a car thus protected
until the signal was removed by the re
pair men. Such a ruie had not been
adopted by the company. The court re
marked that this was a very proper rule,
and if employes were faithful in carrying
it out it would give reasonable protection
to rJpair men. The court held that
whether Buch a rule ought to have been
adopted by the company above mentioned
was a question for the jury to decide.
Repairing Cars.
Under the first head may be mentioned
the case of Morgan against the Hudson
River Ore Company. The company had
ten kilns for roasting ore standing in a
row about ten feet apart. They stood on
a slight decline, and a narrow-gauge rail
road track ran along by the side of the
kilns, upon which cars loaded with ore
were moved by horsepower. One day th<?
finger of an employe was caueht in one
of the wheels of a cur and crushed.
There were no written or printed rules
for conducting the business, though it
was proved that the superintendent had
given some oral instructions with respect
to doing the work. It was contended that
a set of rules ought to have been made
for conducting the business, and that if
this had been done the accident would not
have happened. The court thought other
wise. "There was nothing in the nature
of the business," said the court, "that
made it necessary for the" company to
make and publish rules. The business
had been conducted in the same way for
years before and no 'accident had hap
pened, and none could reasonably be ap
prehended or anticipated. Even if it
could be shown after the accident occur
red that it might have been prevented by
adopting and enforcing some suitable
rule, that would constitute no proper test
of liability. The failure to adopt rules is
not proof of negligence, unless it appears
from the nature of the business in which
the servant is engaged that the master, in
the exercise of reasonable care, should
have foreseen and anticipated the neces
sity of such precautions."
When Is a Rule Necessary?
We start out then with the simple and
reasonable rule that an employer must
make proper rules and t?ive them due pub
licity. This rule, however, at once holds
within Itself the following questions: In
what cases are any rules or regulations
whatever required for the safety of em
ployes? for surely they are not required
in all. Again, in what cases are the cxÂ«
lstlng rules proper and suitable?
In view or the wanderings of ths? courts
in trying to answer the question who are
fellow-servants, we are not convinced by
the learned judge of judicial infallibility.
It is true, that the tinal court in a State
may make a rule which other courts in
that State must follow, but the final court
might go as far astray in doing this as
a jury. Surely all of those who are floun
dering in the hopeless sea of decialons on
the question of fellow-service cannot bo
righjâ nay, some of them must be clearly
and entirely wrong. And indeed on the
question immediately before us their dif
ferences are so ?reat that the prospect or
"eventual uniformity," hopefully predict
ed by Justice Strong, seems to us darkly
remote.
Judicial "Wanderings.
Â¦â¢There are strung reasons," says Mr.
Justice Strong, "why the reasonableness
of railroad regulations should in tne ab
sence cf any pcsltlve proof as to their ef
fect be submitted to tne court as a ques
tion of law, rather than to the jury as
cne of fact. Ordinarily Jurors are not
aware of all the reasons calling for the
rule. They arc apt to listen readily to
any allegations ot injuries on railways.
Wnat ope Jury might deem an incon
venient rule another might approve as
judicious and proper, 'mere would be
no uniformity. Ordinarily the managers
of railways are much better qualined than
Jurors to ascertain and weign the exigen
cies which call for^any rule, and, if it
should be productive of some inconveni
ence, to compare that with the evil to bo
prevented or the good to be produced by
Â»ts adoption. If the question should bo
deemed one of law, there would be event
ual uniformity, as any diversity of senti
ment In the courts below would be finally
harmonized in the court of last resort.'"
Whether an employer ought to make a
particular rule or set of rules in most
states is a Question lor the acclsion of
the court and not of the Jury; in other
words. It is a question of law and not a
question of fact. fcÂ»o also is tho question
wnether a rule 13 reasonable or not.
Not every business requires rules to be
made for the guidance of employes. But
when the business is large and complex,
like that of railroads, lactorif-s, mlr.es
and the like, in which targe nuninÂ«rs are
employed, then the auty 01 making such
lUitij us Will give re&doa&btO. protection 10
employes cieariy^ exists ana siiuutd not
be uisitgarded. â¢'
ulations.

What is an employer'3 duty In the way
of giving instrucf.ons to his employes?
To answer this question fully our Inquiry
may be divided Into three branches The
firat of these relates to his duty to make
end set forth rules and regulations for the
better working of his machinery, appli
ances or enterprise, and also for the
greater safety of his employes. Many of
these rules, as we shall Boon learn, are
prepared more especially for the benefit of
youthful and inxperlenced persona.

An Illinois Case.
A case has arisen In Illinois of a switch
man who on a dark night was struck, by
a car and killed wh;ie attempting to
reach a switch in obedience to a signal.
It appeared, that the switch was danger
ous and that the company had adopted
no rules with respect to switching. Tho
switch was known &s a "flying switch,"
some of the cars also had defective
brakes and there was no light on the
front car, though it was usual to put ono
there on dark nights. One of the wit
nesses for the company testified that he
never saw any rules about flying switches
and did not think any one else had. Yet
he also said Immediately afterward that
three whistles wtre used as a signal for
the switch. "By this," said the court,
"we understand It was by fore* of a pre
scribed rule these signals werÂ» made. Im
portant to be made, certainly, and so Im
portant that a railroad company would
te Justly charged with negligence in not
prescribing anct enforcing them. It Is per
fectly feasible to prescribe definite rules
for making flying switches, though they
may be seldom made."
These are a few of the many answers
to the question. In what cases should
rules or regulations be made for conduct-
Ing a business more safely? The other
question, whether the rule in a given case
is a reasonable one or not, can evidently
be answered only when it arises. A larga
number of cases have happened which
turned on the answer to this question.
Were the rules existing for governing: em
ployes reasonable or sufficient? This, as
we have said, is In most States a question
of law for the court to decide. In one of
the cases, relating to a railroad. It was
held that a rule which merely stated
"Blue is a signal to be used by car In
spectors" was not sufficiently definite.
In another case a rule required car con
ductors to look after switches when used
by their engineer. This was held to be a
sufficient rule, consequently the company
was not liable for the death of a con
ductor who was killed through the neglect
cf another who knew the rule but neglect
ed to adjust a switch properly.
ALBERT S. BOLLES.
An accident happened to a railroad em
ploye in Kansas, who was injured while
attempting to couple cars from a de
tached car. The company had no estab
lished rukÂ» or custom for notifying or
warning' s'j^chmfn wnrkiner In the com

cars, cxcjit that thÂ«Â» boll of an engine
