pany was miilisfnt in not having: some
regulatien about the movinp of such cars.
Tho Supreme Court decided that a Jury
was not justified in finding that such a
regulation would be feasible or useful.
An important case of this kind has b**en
decided by the Supreme Court of Missouri.
A boy seventeen year3 old wag employed
in carrying water to a gansr of men who
were repairing the roadbed, and in look
ing after the tools. He. with the fore
man and other laborers, traveled In a ca
boose car attached to a fretsht train.
They alighted at a place and left the train
standing on the main track. The fore
man, with the boy. went a short distance
along- the track, and then the boy was
sent back to the. caboose to notify the la
borers about their work and assist them
in getting out their tools. After doing

was knocked -off and the caboose ran over
h!s arm and leg, badly injuring him. The
cars were moved by the engineer arut
brakeman. "by the maneuver known as
'kicking-.' " As the engineer and brake
man were fellow-servants with the boy â
all working in the same service â he could
not recover for their negligence, however
great: hi? only chance was to show that
the company was negligent "In falling and
omitting to provide any rules or regula
tions or system" in such cases. The lower
court rendered a verdict in favor of the
company, but the Court of Review re
garded the matter differently, remarking
that one who employs servants In a com
plex and dangerous r.uslness ought to pre
scribe proper rule3 for conducting It safe
ly. The railroad contended that In Joln
injr cars, especially in the case of freight
trains. It did not Involve any unusual risk.
"We do not," said the court, "agree to the
proposition. Tt is certainly a complex
business requiring care, and must be dan
gerous, if not done under proper regula
tions, at least to far as other servants
are concerned, whose business requires
them to be in and out of the cars, liable
to be jolted. In the case of shunting or
kicking cars, it is feasible and perfectly
proper to have some rules and regulations
to warn persons liable to be Injured, and
c^ses are not wanting where railroad
companies have been held liable to ser
vants for injuries? received in consequence
of a vrant of such regulations for tho
guidance of the servants In performing
these maneuvers."

AHEEST ON BELGIAN KING.
Three Chinese Locked Up in the Har
bor Police Station.
Ah Bo, a waiter on the United States
transport Belgian King, will have to an
swer to a charge of grand larceny. When
the steamship reached port Dr. -R. Colt
man reported that he had lost S1S5 in
greenbacks. Three Chinese, who fre
quently entered his quarters, were suspect
ed, but there was no proof against them.
Finally when Detective Egan found the
men trying to leave the ship he placed
them under arrest. When searched at the
police station $175 in greenbacks was found
sewed into the lining of Ah Bo's clothes.
When taxed with the theft he admitted
having found the monev in Dr. Coltman's
room and said the missing $10 he had paid
to the chief steward on account of a debt.
Ah Bo's confession cleared 1 the other Chi
r.ese and they were released. Ah Bo was
then charged with grand larceny.

comes here in ballast and will take back

and general merchandise. 1 The Bering

Â» FTER taking a year, all but nine
i\ days, to round the Horn, the
ZA\ French bark Eugenia Fautrel
finally made i>ort yesterday.
This is her maiden voyage and
on this year's venture the owners will
have to face a dead loss. A defective rud
der caused all the trouble and to it the
captain lays the blame of a six months'
detention at Buenos Ayres.
The Eugenia Fautrel left Swansea ex
actly 256 days ago. On January 23 sho
was in latitude 11 scuth. longituae 3s west.
On March TJ sne was in ijuenos Ayres
with her rudder broken and otherwise
aaniaged. The ve^el was Duilt under the
new French subsidy law and as the own
ers wanted her in a hurry she was built
under "rush orders." She is a handsome
looKingr craft, but when the test came In
a pampero off. the River Platte she was
not equal to the occasion. When the
storm was over the Eugenia Fautrel was
almost helpless. Her steering gear was
disabled and the rudder broken nearly in
two. During- the calm that followed the
storm a jury steering gear and rudder
were fixed up and the bark finally reached
Buenos Ayres.
An soon as port was reached the real
trouble began. The vessel was surveyed
and on April 2 orders were sent to France
for a new rudder. It was nearly five
months before the necessary articles ar
rived and it was September 19 before the
Eugenia Fautrel was ready to sail again.
After getting away from Buenos Ayres
she ran Into a succession of calms, so the
captaln put into Montevideo In the hope
of picking up some men as the vessel was
snort-handed, five of the crew having de
serted while at Buenos Ayres.
At Montevideo they found the British
bark Mistley Hall. She had been in a
hurricane and was completely dismasted.
Her decks were swept and three of the
sailors had lost their lives. Two of them
were blown from the yard nrm into the
sea and one fell from aloft and was In
stantly killed. The Mistley Hall was such
a. wreck that the owners decided to sell
her. The crew wanted to get home, so
live of tlvrn shipped on the Frenchman.
All of them are now anxious to get ashore
and the chances are that by this morning
the bark will be minus five of her crew.
Since the Mistley Hall disaster was re
ported the bark Auldgerth has been to
Montevideo and taken aboard her cargo.
The latter vessel Is now fifty days out
on her return to San Francisco with the
Mistley Hall's cargo, while the remains
of the latter vessel have been sold to an
Italian firm.

Weather Report.
"Oraiures have matured rapidly In Southern
California, but In oth^r sections their develop

vailing in Southern California is causing fruit
fcufis to swell and there Is said to be some
apprehension cf damage to next season's crop

that section."

very profperous season.

tdjustment of anthracite affairs Involved in
the abandonment of this plan would be of great
tapcrtance. as was plainly shown by the buoy
ancy of all the stocks in the group. No author
itative confirmation could be had of the re
ports current, but the record of the tape was
Btrongly confirmatory, the gains In the group
running from 1 to 3 points. There was some
demand from London on account of the open-
Ing cf a new account Incident to the settle
ment, but this buying is called professional
arbitrage operation and may originate either
in New York or in London. The most marked
effect was produced on Southern Pacific, which
opened up 1U and advanced later to 43, clos
ing at the highest. The belief has prevailed
in 'Wall street that there was . a very heavy
ehort account in some of the railroad stocks
for which Western speculators were liable,
the Epeculation extending also Into the steel
stocks. The bear account In the latter troup
has been aimed at by the advance this weÂ«k
and may have been reached In the railroad
group this afternoon.
The money market outlook continued threat
ening, so that the supposition is aroused that
to-day's buying is for the account of financial
interests which are independent of the' ordi
nary resources of the money market. The sub
treasury has already taken from the market
this week the sum of $2,2OS,O00. so that the
Inroad on the resources of the banks promises
to be as great this week as lt was last week.
The London money market was not affected
to-day by yesterday's sharp drop in the ster
ling exchange here and exchange rates fell
a further fraction. This may foreshadow an
acquiescence in the New York demand for gold.
The ehlpment from India to London of $2,500,
000 in gold in payment for silver may be in
tercepted and diverted to the New York mar
ket, as was done with the last similar ship
ment. but Its arrival in New York would be
too remote to affect the present situation, even
if lt were ascertained. Whatever the motive,
the strength in stocks was well maintained to
the close, except in the steel group.
The bond market was irregular. Total sales
per value. $4,045,000.

i Harrison's circular eaya: "Since th* Marl
posa left the following coal-laden vessels havÂ«
arrived from Australia, viz.: Invercoe, 230O
tons; Drumburton. 2363; Thistlebank, 3870;
total. S933 tons. Since the Newcastle product
has lost its local prestige here, on account of
the shrinkage in Its shipments and its high cost
delivered here, in all contracts now made for
future fuel by our large consumers colonial coal
is entirely eliminated. Of course there will
bo a limited demand annually for Newcastle
coal, as it is preferred by some to the northern
fuel, even at enhanced prloes, although the
present existing difference is large. The local
call for coal at Newcastle and Sydney Is now
so brisk that but Hula attention Is given to
this market, as the product of the principal
cclllerles Is sold into the middle of 190L Brit
ish Columbia and Washington are keeping us
gtnerously supplied, so that there will be no
danger of a scarcity this winter of either steam
or domestic grades. There is a marked dlffer
erce In the asking prices of these, as the pro
ducers of the best qualities of house fuel are
limited in numbers and they do not overload
the market with their output, hence values are
well sustained. Next year crude oil will be
delivered here in large quantities and compe
tition will be so sharp that low figures must
prevail. Where fuel figures largely in the
idonthly expenses of some of our factories oil
will necessarily come to their rescue and will
cause the reopening of a large number of
others."

Dirby Laydon appeared for sentence In
the United States District. Court yester
day morning-, he having been convicted
by a Jury of a violation of the Federal
statute prohibiting the working of men
on Government contracts for more than
eight hours per day. His attorney, P. F.
Dunne, asked for a new trial and the re
quest was denied. Judge de Haven then
pronounced sentence. Imposing a fine of
$200. A stay of ten days was granted.

Jerry Dolan and his wife Mary had a
dispute at the breakfast table yesterday
morning which culminated in his kicking
her. She fell to .the floor and he left the
house and went to his work as a teamster
with C. B. Bode. Later on during "the
morning Mrs. Dolan's neighbors heard
her moaning, and on going to her aid
they found her lying on the floor unable
to rise. The ambulance was sent for and
the woman removed from her home at 33
Victor street- to' the Emergency Hospital.
After the broken limb was set she was
removed to the City and County Hospi
tal. Dolan was arrested later in the day.
Charged With, Breaking Wife's Leg.
Doctors Admitted to Bail.
Judge Conlan . decided yesterday that
Drs. G. W. O'Donnell and E. A. Bohm
charged with the murder of Mm Annie
Giddings should be admitted to bail.
O'Donnell's bonds were fixed at $10 000
and Bohm's at $5000. O'Donnell at once
gave bonds, his sureties being his wife,
Mrs. Elizabeth O'Donnell, and his father
Dr. C. C. O'Donnell. Bohm is still in
prison.
Alvln Demuth, the portrait painter, at
2131 O'Farrell street, who bared his wife's
throat and played around It with a razor
for fifteen minutes threatening to kill her
again appeared before Judge Conlan yes
terday. The Judge ordered him to keep
the peace in $1000 bonds, and failing to
provide the bonds he was sentto Jail.

was accused of an assault with a deadly
weapon, had the case dismissed by Judge

Two Vacancies on. Comxnission,
Governor Gage and the president of the
State Board of Agriculture were in con
sultation yesterday in reference to the
selection of a permanent secretary of the
board. While this appointment is one of
the privileges of the board, It will, by
consent, be exercised by Governor Gage
and the meeting yesterday was held to
learn the wishes of the Governor. Gage
Is not yet ready to announce his decision
and will not be until hÂ« fills two vacancies
which will occur in the membership of
the board in February. Through the offi
cers whom he. will- then appoint he will
indicate his choice for secretary.
This important position Is now held tem
porarily by Peter J. Shields, a newly
elected Superior Judge of Sacramento.
When Mr. Shields was elected to the
bench he was permanent secretary of the
Board of Agriculture. He submitted his
resignation and was j>ersuaded to remain
temporarily in the position.

More . than five hundred people were
present on Sunday at the flagr raising at
tho town of Richmond, the terminus of
the Santa Fe road on San Pablo Bay. In
the short tpace of a few weeks nearly two
hundred persons have located in the new
town, all of whom are employes of the
Santa Fe. I>ast week It was decided to
celebrate the birth of the town by. a flag
raising, and the event was scheduled to
take place last Sunday.
The stars and stripes were hoisted on
Tewksbury track at 2 p. m. and a pleasant
augury of the future was the breaking
of the sun through the heavy fog, which
had hung over the town all the morn
ing.
The flag and pole were donated by Wil
liam Thomas, and F. F. Carlson raised
Old Glory, amid the plaudits of those
present, and a salute or twenty-one guns
was fired by William Southa, Joseph
Numes, O. Brook, J. P. Agree, W. Paris
and F. Torn. Among: the residents of the
town of Richmond who took part In tho
celebration were F. Farrell, George El
mat, W. Bell, W. G. Garreitz, W. Atkin
son, M. Coleman. G. H. Huntinpton. J.
Nunes, W. M. Coan, G. W. LJston. A.

L. Lister. Charles Addington. G. Wick
nash. J. FrateB. Ed Nugent, George Ellis,
W. Halllday, Jerry Edwards and Frank
Edwards.

She Is Granted Decree on Ground of
Infidelity â Other Unhappy
Couples Separate.
Adgie C. Hall, tamer of lions, has di
vorced her husband, Frank R. Hall, whom
she found untamable. Adgle was granted
a decree by Judge Belcher yesterday on
the ground'of infidelity, together. with the
right to resume her maiden name, Cas
tello. Adgie testified that she married her
husband last July, and even before the
honeymoon had waned she found that
she had been enjoying but a portion of
his affections, his attentions being pro
miscuously distributed among several
young women at the Terminal Hotel,
Haight and Stanyan streets. Adgie's tes
timony was corroborated by Mrs. Fannie
Ames. A decree was forthwith granted,
and Adgle, with a smile, returned to her
lions.
Decrees of divorce were also granted to
Lillian M. Pyal from James A. Pyal for
willful neglect, Kate Barnes from Clay
ton Barnes for willful neglect. Birdie M.
Hart from George R. Hart for willful neg
lect Lucy K. Dleo from A. R. Dice for
willful neglect and Esther S. Orchard
from William H. Orchard for willful neg
lect.

MRS. HEARST RESIGNS.
Sabid Utterances of Alexander Horr
Applauded by Hundreds of
His Fellows.
NETT YORK, Dec. 11.â Five hundred
Anarchists vociferously applauded to
r.lght at a meeting on behalf of the fam
ily of Bresd, the assassin, when one of
their speakers threatened the life of Pres
ident McKinley. "should ha attempt to
interfere with free speech." The speaker
was Alexander Horr. who announced his
approval of assassination.
The Anarchists met In Everett Hall. 33
East Fourth street. They had obtained
the hall by a ruse which Harry Bimberg,
the proprietor, resented, and to-night he
made feeble threats to put out the lights.
He thought better of it. however, when
one of the speakers declared they would
go to law about the matter should he
do so.
When Bresei was sentenced for the kill
ing of King Humbert the assassin's wife
iiiid two children were left in need. The
audience to-nifrht took up a collection for
thfiir benefit. Horr made a rabid speech:
"If I were fa Russia to-day," said he, i
"1 should be an assassin. If any one in j
control of the Government here persists j
!ri prohibiting the right of free speech, if
the Mayor of the city does it or the Gov
ernor of tho State or the President of the
United States, he dues it at his peril."

COVELO, Dec 11.â News of an Indian
shooting affray at Laytonville reached
here late this evening. Frank Poe, a
noted desperado. Influenced by the exces
sive use of firewater, rode Into the In
dian camp and commenced shooting at his
wife. To her screams he paid no atten
tion, but followed her Into the house and
fired two shots at her there. The noise
and screams of some twenty Indians soon
brought Deputy Sheriff Woodruff and
Constable McAnnich to their assistance.
Frank was relieved of his weapon and in
a short time was looking through the bars
of the Town Jail. This morning when
Poe awakened he asked If he had killed
his wife, and when told that she was all
right he said he wanted to kill her be
cause she had fallen In love with an old
medicine man by the name of Kabaso.
Attempts to Kill His "Wife.

Local coal men say that other San Fran
cisco dealers have been making similar
endeavors to increase their coal receipts.
Washington coal mines are now producing
more coal than ever before. Old mines
are furnishing their maximum output and
new properties are being opened. Local
railroads, factories and steamers are tak
ing double the quantity of coal they used
three years ago. Besides this, the enor
mous demand from Alaska and the Ha
waiian Islands is now being supplied here.
All the coal used by the northern rail
roads west of Montana comes from. Puget
Sound mines. Considering these facts,
local dealers say that the supply Is likely
to fall far short of the demand all winter.
By another year they think the mines will
catch' up.
- . i i
TACOMA, Dec 11.â Coal prices In Saa
Francisco will be advanced again soon,
according to a statement made here to
day by C. B. Fan-ell, a coal dealer of
that city. He came north several days
ago to ascertain if the mines could not
furnish more coal for immediate ship
ment, recent receipts at San Francisco
having been shorter than usual. He finds
that the mines now have all the orders
they canjKÂ»Esibly take carÂ© of, and that
even if more coal were available there are
not enough ships and steamers in sight to
carry it to California. On these facts ha
bases his statement that another price
advance Is likely to follow on both steam
and domestic coals.

THE HAGUE, J3ec 11.â Replying in the
Second Chamber to-day to the Interpella
tion of Count von Bylandt. notice bf
which was given yesterday, on the sub
ject of the tension betwen The Nether
lands and Portugal, the Foreign Min
ister. Dr. W. IL de Beaufort, sketched
the history of tho misunderstanding-,
which, he declared, had been exaggerated.
He said the Dutch Minister at Lisbon,
Baron von Heekeren, announced on No
vember 17 that Portugal desired to with
draw the exequatur of Herr Pott as Con
sul of The Netherlands at Lourenzo Mar
ques, unless the Government of The
Netherlands would obviate the necessity
by dismissing or recalling Herr Pott. As
The Netherlands Government had no of
ficial information that Herr Pott had per
mitted, as alleged, the importation of
contraband of war, it would not accede to
the demand for his recall without a fuller
inquiry and it therefore instructed the
Minister at Lisbon to cable to Herr Pott
asking him to clear up the matter. Herr
Pott then applied for leave to come to Eu
rope, which was granted him. and The
Netherlands proposed to Portugal that an
Investigation be made during the Consul's
visit and that three months be allowed
for the rendering of a report on the sub
ject.

bunals.

cinnati, and many political clubs and

Charles M. Hays, the new president of
the Southern Pacific, is to accompany
"Vice President J. C. Stubbs when he re
turns to this city from the East the lattei
part of the month. At least that is the
understanding among the officials of the
company here. H. E. Huntlngton may
also return at the same time. He is now
In New York, where he went last week,
ostensibly to dispose of the Huntingdon
estate"s Southern Padnc shares. Mr.
Stubbs is now in Washington with W.
F. Hcrrin attending a meeting of the In
terstate Commerce Commission, which is
to pass upon the case of the St. Louis
Merchnnts' Acsociation against the South
trn Pacific and Santa Fe roads.
Both Mr. Stubbs and Mr. Hays were
present at a meeting of the Western rail
way presidents in New York last week,
and Mr. Stubbs met the Southern Pacific's
new president on that occasion for the
tlrst time. At this meeting a proposition
to discontinue the practice of Issuing
passes to employes of railroad lines wa3
discussed and a special committee was ap
pointed to tÂ»ke the matter up with all
the Eastern and Southern lines and re
port some plan of action for the year 1902.
In the opinion of the conference so radical
a change in old customs as is proposed
cannot De made in less than a year.

Shorter High School Course.
Those pupils who will be promoted from
the advanced eighth grade of the public
schools to the high schools at the end of
this month will take a three-and-one-half
>ear course In the high M:hools. This was
decided upon at the executive session of
the Hoard of Education yesterday. Eight
schools will send entire advanced eighth
grade classes up for the examination.
NO CERTIFICATE
FOR NEW THEATER
Playhouse.
One for Central Park
Board of Works Will Refuse
Supervisors Hay Rescind Ordinance
Which Does Not Include Priv
ilege of Using Building for
Theatrical Purposes.
In all probability the promoters of the
new Central Park Theater venture win be
unable to open the doors of their play
house to the public. While the Board of
Public Works has as yet taken no official
action. Commissioner Manson intimated
yesterday that a certificate of proper con
struction would not be forthcoming. The
Tax Collector will refuse to issue a license
on that score.
The attention of the Board of Super
visors has been called to these facts by
Mayor Phelan and as a result the Ju
diciary Committee will on next Friday
consider the rescinding of the resolution
granting the privilege of altering and
erecting additions to the old panorama
building on Market street, near Eighth.
One point that may result In the rescind
ing of the permit is that the resolution
does not grant the privilege of using the
altered building for theatrical purposes,
but for amusement purposes only. The
ordinance merely says:
Resolved, That the Central ParJc Amusement
Company be and Is hereby granted permission
to build in Central Park, corner Eighth and
Market streets, a gravity railway; also to con
struct and maintain a temporary butldlnS of
Iron, with wooden frame, adjoining rear of
present Iron covered bulltilnir, in said park; the
whole to be used for amusement purposes, and
the privilege to continue at the pleasure of the
board.
Commissioner Manson says that the
resolution was passed In July, or Just one
month after the passage of the ordinance
regulating the construction and mainte
nance of theaters. He says that the reso
lution allowing the alterations to the
building was passed by the Supervisors In
direct conflict with the theater ordinance.
When the attention of the board was
called to thf fact that the building was
being reconstructed in violation of the fire
ordinance, it decirt/nl that the permit
could not be rescinded. It held that It
was the duty of tne Fire E>epartment to
see that the alterations were made ac
cording to law. Fire Chief Sullivan con
tended that under the charter the rÂ«rpon
plbility did not lie with his department
and declined to accept it. The Board of
Public Works also refused to take any
action until an application was made by
the Central Theater proprietors for a cer
tificate. The Board of Works will hold.
Manson says, that the covering of gal
vanized iron on wooden partitions is rn
conflict with both the theater and nre
ordinances and a certificate will be re
fused. Before that happens, however.
Manson hopes that the Supervisors will
crawl out of their dilemma In having Ille
gally granted the privilege by rescinding
the resolution on the subject.
JOHN EABDEB WAS
PROBABLY MTTRDERED
Mutilated Body of the Seattle Man
Found Near the Ocean
Dock.
SEATTLE. Dec. 11.â The mystery sur-
rounding the strange disappearance of
John Harder, who was last seen alive
Tuesday tight, November 19, was solved
to-day by the finding of his body In the
bay near the ocean dock. Every appear
ance points to Harder having been mur
dered. On his head were several wounds,
probably made by a blunt instrument
from behind. The trousers pockets were
turned lrside out. showing that he had
been robbed.
What lends further color to the murder
theory is the fact that Harder was seen
early In the morning after leaving hl3
work, at a txÂ»er hall on Second street, in
company with a strange man, who said
he came from Montana.
PIONEEB JAMES BROWN DEAD.
Well Known, to Early Settlers of Cal
ifornia and Nevada.
SPOKANE. Dec. 11.â The funeral of
James Brown, better known to California
and Xevada pioneers as "Kettlebelly"
Brown, took place here to-day. Brown
was a pioneer on the coastâ a forty-niner.
In early days in Virginia City, when Mark
Twain and Dan de QutU lived there.
Brown was the chief of the Fire E>epart
ment. He had lived here the last twelve
years.
An old resident of California here who
had known Brown In his earlier days saw
to it that he did not suffer for food or
shelter. He was 78 years Of age and, so
far as known, left no relatives.

THE DAY'S DEAD.
J. J. Brecker.
RENO, Dec. 11.â About two weeks ago
J. J. Brecker, one of the oldest and most
highly respected residents of Reno, wns
found in the rear of his establishment in
an unconscious condition. He was con
veyed at once to his home and medical aid
summoned, but without avail, and to-day
at 12:40 he passed away. He leaves a wife,
two sons and three daughters. His estate
is estimated at between $60,000 and $70,000.

WAII'S DELEGATE-ELECT.
defendant if he formerly owned a coupling
pin which he produced. Hoff gave an eva

The -prosecution will close its side of
the case by noon to-day.
COUPLING PIN
IS IN EVIDENCE
"PleasÂ«d to Meet You," Says
Seymour When Ha First
Meets Hoff.
The afternoon session of the Hoff case
yesterday was replete with both dramatic
and humorous situations. Captain of De
tectives Seymour was on the stand and
told of his search for Hoff the day after
the Clute murder and how he eventually
found him in Captain Lees* office in the
City Hall and was formally introduced to
him. After the presentation Seymour says
that he said to Hoff, "I ant pleased to
meet you. Mr. Hoff." There was a ripple
of laughter in the courtroom when Sey
mour made this admission, and nobody
doubted that he was glad to meet Hoff
after a twenty-four hour fruitless search
for the much-wanted man.
The dramatic feature of the day was
when Dr. E. Bunnell was on the stand.
He grasped the coupling pin firmly In hla
hand and showed the jury how a terrible
blow could be inflicted with the weapon
and how it might leave a wound in the
hand of the person who held it.
Â¦ Yesterday morning the first witness was
Police Officer Feeney. Ho concluded his
testimony. of the day before. He waa fol
lowed by Sergeant of Police H. H. Colby,
who stated that Hoff appeared at police
headquarters, on the | morning, after the
murder about 8 o'clock. He asked for the
Chief of Police, and said that he was tho
carpet layer that: they were looking for
and that his name was Hoff. Colby sent
for the official stenographer and Captain
Seymour.. Hoff then made a statement to

If his divorced wife carries out her plans
to oppose him. Roberts was rejected by
Congress, who fourd him guilty of con
tracting bigamous marriage, and Wilcox's
case is identical with that of Roberts.
The white population of the Hawaiian
Islands Is determined, that Wilcox shall
not represent their interest in Congress,
and every effort is being made to prevent
him doing so. There can be little doubt
aa to the action Congress will take In the
contest, for the case of Brigham Roberts
forms a precedent which is not likely to
be set aside.
Wilcox, who is a "half-white." won the
prince&s as his bride when he was a mili
tary student In Italy and the protege of
tho late King of Hawaii. The blandish
ments and roseate stories he painted had
a rude awakening when the couple
reached their island home in 1S97. Dis
gusted with her husband's actions, the
young princess fled to this city, where her
child was born. Wilcox followed her and
for a time they were in the direst straits.
Wilcox went back to Hawaii and his wife
was assisted to return to her home in
Italy. Four years ago she filed suit for
annulment of the marriage In the Italian
courts, and shortly afterward Wilcox
married the Princess Koehelelani, a de
scendant of the Kamehamehas, despite
the fact that his Italian wife had not yet
secured her legal freedom. Since Wilcox
and his Italian wife separated she has
always expressed the most bitter feelings
toward him, and if she should come to
Washington to protest against his being
seated in Congress, it Is satfe to assume
that she will not decorate him with "leis"
or welcome him with "alohas."
Judgment is thereupon given for the
plaintiff for $2240. less-JCOO, for which the
stock was sold, and the $120 interest to de
fendant, leaving a remainder of J1520 with
6 per cent Interest on that sum from Oc
tober 31. 1S94.
Section 6201. of the Revised Statutes of the
T-nlted States prohibits banks from dealing
In their stock except to protect themselves
against debts, and while attention has not been
called to any statute directly prohibiting what
these parties did, it Is clearly against the
spirit of the law to make such contracts as
they attempted to carrv out. To allow bank
officers to deal In the stock of the bank be
tween themselves, and to make such contracts
as the parties attempted to make In thlf
caee, would opÂ»Â»n the door to innumerable
frauds. The sale of the stock by the defend
ant to the bank cannot be sustained. The
bank, however, subsequently sold the stock for
J600. and as It had the upe of the defendant's
JTOOO for a year, he should be allowed for that
use the legal Interest of 6 per rent, equaling
$120, but he is not entitled to the $120 for al
leged salary, the allowance of that having
been a inert pretext to pay him 12 per cent
Interest as agreed.
On October 25. 1893. the defendant purchased
of the bank twenty shares of its stock, for
which be paid J2000, with the agreement be
tween him and the president and cashier of
the bank that at any time within a year he
could return the stock and be repaid the pur
chase price and 12 per cent interest thereon.
Thereupon he was elected a director and vice
president of the bank, and at the end of the
year he returned the stock and the eame o..
oere received It and paid him therefor $2349
but instead of paying him 12 per cent Inter
est they allowed him 6 per cent, amounting
to tl20, and allowed him the additional $130 for
salary as vice president. Notwithstanding his
surrender of the stock, he continued for more
than a year to act as director and vice presi
dent, the stock In the meantime remaining
upon the books of the bank In his name. Sub
sequently the bank sold the stock for $600.

The only conditions upon which I would

of the board of trustees by admitting thereto

According to Information later received
by Dr. Potter this communication never
reached the faculty. It was presented to
the faculty meeting and by a aeries of
parliamentary tactics was placed on the
table without being read or receiving the
slightest consideration. On November 13
Dr. Potter received a set of complimen
tary resolutions as follows:
College of Physicians and Burgeons
SAN FRANCISCO, Cal.. Nov. 13, 1900.
Dr. 8. O. L. Potter, San Francisco, Cal.â
Dear Doctor: By direction of the medical fac
ulty of the College of Phyaicians and Surgeons,
I tore the honor to present to you the follow
ing resolutions adopted by the faculty at a
meeting held November 7, 1900.
Under these circumstances I cannot consent
to have any further association with a body
which has absolutely ignored a respectful and
eerious set of charges made by one of its
members against its management bf the affairs
of the Institution. The .facts above recited
speak for themselves and will no doubt com
mend to your Judgment the position taken by
me. My self-respect would not permit me to
continue a member of the board after my re
monstrance was treated with silent contempt.
In regard to my continuing in the faculty I
beg to say that with the present restricted
membership of the board of trustees the finan
cial and educational status of the college Is
not sufficiently guarded or conserved. As the
board is now constituted four men can rule
the Institution without hindrance and can
spend its earnings (188,000 in four years) with
out question.
Its educational and professional standards
are being steadily lowered by tne admission
of students of known bad antecedents, some
having been given two years' advanced stand
ing though not entitled to any such. The
faculty Is steadily and 'deliberately Ignored In
the management of the college; its standing
committees are not arpolnted and the powers
originally given to It have been usurped by
the officers of the board of trustees. No col
lego can last long in this country under such
arbitrary management as this and I cannot
lend whatever Influence I have among students
and in the medical profession, to support a
policy which I do not approve or to sustain
educational methods which I have strenuously
opp'osed all my life.
On the 17th of last month I presented to your
board of trustees, of which I was then a
member, a formal remonstrance concerning cer
tain changes In policy and certain faulty acts
of administration in the management of the
college. A copy of this remonstranca Is ap
pended hereto for your Information. I pre
sented It in person, at a meeting called for
the purpose of hearing a committee appointed
some time previously on the finances of the
college and to suggest Improvements in its ad
ministration. I was a member of that com
mittee, but I did not report as such, for the
reason that I found my coadjutor adverse to
my chief recommendation,' namely, the Increase
of the board of trustees to fifteen members.
Hence I made an Individual report. I with
drew immediately after presenting It, saying
that I did Â«o In order to enable the board to
discuss It freely, unembarrassed by my pres
ence. The paper waa received and read at
that meeting. 1 waited ten days for some
official request to substantiate my statements
or for some conference by committee or other
wise concerning the serious charge* brought
by me against the management of the college.
Hearing nothing whatever In that time I sent
my resignation to the beard on the 27th ultimo,
and this was acknowledged by the secretary
pro tem. under date of September 28, saying
that both papers would be taken up at" the
regular meeting on October 1. On October 3
I received the formal acceptance of my resig
nation. Taut no mention then or since of my
remonstrance or of any action by the board
thereon.
Immediately following Dr. Potter's res
ignation a committee of the faculty, of
which Dr. E. E. Kelley was the chair
man, waited upon Dr. Potter and asked
him to withdraw his resignation, state
his reasons therefor and remain, at least
a professor emeritus. Dr. Potter made
repiy and duly transmitted the following
letter, together with a copy of the report
to the board of trustees, which has al
ready been made public:
1220 SUTTER STREET,
BAN FRANCISCO, Cal.. Oct. 29, 1900.
To the Medical Faculty. College Physicians
and Surgeons of San Franciscoâ Gentlemen : I
nave the honor to acknowledge your request,
made by your committee, that I would con
tinue my connection in some manner with the
College of Physicians and Surgeons. While
I am flattered by this mark of your confidence
I feel that I cannot make any proper answer
thereto without Informing you fully of the
reasons for my resignation and the circum
stances attending it.
The trustees desire me to say that they regret
exceedingly that you have seen fit to take this
step, that they have always entertained and
dp now entertain for you a very high degree of
respect, have considered you a most excellent
teacher, a safe counselor and a true friend,
and that your resignation has been accepted
only because they believed that it was offered
with sincerity and that no measures which they
might adopt would be sufficient to change your
purpose. They desire me to extend to you
their very best wishes for your health and
prosperity, and hope that in severing your con
nection with the college they have not lost a
friend. Tours truly, D. A. HODOHEAD.
Secretary Pro Tem.
